PART ONE

Chapter I: The Bertolini

``The Signora had no business to do it,'' (Bertolini/Signora/Signora Bertolini)
said Miss Bartlett,
``no business at all. She (it) promised us south rooms with a view close together, instead of which here are north rooms, looking into a courtyard, and a long way apart. Oh, Lucy!'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

``And a Cockney, besides!'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)
said Lucy, who had been further saddened by the Signora's unexpected accent.
``It might be London.'' (She)
She (Lucy) looked at the two rows of English people who were sitting at the table; at the row of white bottles of water and red bottles of wine that ran between the English people; at the portraits of the late Queen and the late Poet Laureate that hung behind the English people, heavily framed; at the notice of the English church -LRB-Rev. Cuthbert Eager, M. A. Oxon.-RRB-, that was the only other decoration of the wall.
``Charlotte, don't you feel, too, that we might be in London? I can hardly believe that all kinds of other things are just outside. I suppose it is one's being so tired.'' (unknown)

``This meat has surely been used for soup,'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)
said Miss Bartlett, laying down her (Bartlett) fork.

``I want so to see the Arno. The rooms the Signora promised us in her (Charlotte) letter would have looked over the Arno. The Signora had no business to do it at all. Oh, it is a shame!'' (unknown)

``Any nook does for me,'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)
Miss Bartlett continued;
``but it does seem hard that you shouldn't have a view.'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

Lucy felt that she (Lucy) had been selfish.

``Charlotte, you mustn't spoil me: of course, you must look over the Arno, too. I meant that. The first vacant room in the front--'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)
``You must have it,'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)
said Miss Bartlett, part of whose travelling expenses were paid by Lucy's mother--a piece of generosity to which she (Lucy) made many a tactful allusion.

``No, no. You must have it.'' (unknown)

``I insist on it. Your mother would never forgive me, Lucy.'' (unknown)

``She (Lucy) would never forgive me.'' (The ladies)

The ladies' voices grew animated, and--if the sad truth be owned--a little peevish. They were tired, and under the guise of unselfishness they wrangled. Some of their neighbours interchanged glances, and one of them--one of the ill-bred people whom one does meet abroad--leant forward over the table and actually intruded into their argument. He (neighbours) said:

``I have a view, I have a view.'' (He)

Miss Bartlett was startled. Generally at a pension people looked them over for a day or two before speaking, and often did not find out that they would

``do'' (a pension people)
till they had gone. She (Bartlett) knew that the intruder was ill-bred, even before she (Bartlett) glanced at him (intruder). He (intruder) was an old man, of heavy build, with a fair, shaven face and large eyes. There was something childish in those eyes, though it was not the childishness of senility. What exactly it was Miss Bartlett did not stop to consider, for her (Bartlett) glance passed on to his (intruder) clothes. These did not attract her (Bartlett). He (intruder) was probably trying to become acquainted with them before they got into the swim. So she (Bartlett) assumed a dazed expression when he (intruder) spoke to her (Bartlett), and then said:
``A view? Oh, a view! How delightful a view is!'' (her)

``This is my son,'' (the man)
said the old man;
``his (son) name's George. He (son) has a view too.'' (the man)

``Ah,'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)
said Miss Bartlett, repressing Lucy, who was about to speak.

``What I mean,'' (he)
he (man) continued,
``is that you can have our rooms, and we'll have yours. We'll change.'' (he)

The better class of tourist was shocked at this, and sympathized with the new-comers. Miss Bartlett, in reply, opened her (Bartlett) mouth as little as possible, and said

``Thank you very much indeed; that is out of the question.'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

``Why?'' (the man)
said the old man, with both fists on the table.

``Because it is quite out of the question, thank you.'' (unknown)

``You see, we don't like to take--'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)
began Lucy. Her (Lucy) cousin again repressed her (Lucy).

``But why?'' (he)
he (cousin) persisted.
``Women like looking at a view; men don't.'' (he)
And he (cousin) thumped with his (cousin) fists like a naughty child, and turned to his (cousin) son, saying,
``George, persuade them!'' (son)

``It's so obvious they should have the rooms,'' (the son)
said the son.
``There's nothing else to say.'' (He)

He (son) did not look at the ladies as he (son) spoke, but his (son) voice was perplexed and sorrowful. Lucy, too, was perplexed; but she (Lucy) saw that they were in for what is known as

``quite a scene,'' (she)
and she (Lucy) had an odd feeling that whenever these ill-bred tourists spoke the contest widened and deepened till it dealt, not with rooms and views, but with--well, with something quite different, whose existence she (Lucy) had not realized before. Now the old man attacked Miss Bartlett almost violently: Why should she (Bartlett) not change? What possible objection had she (Bartlett)? They would clear out in half an hour.

Miss Bartlett, though skilled in the delicacies of conversation, was powerless in the presence of brutality. It was impossible to snub any one so gross. Her (Bartlett) face reddened with displeasure. She (Bartlett) looked around as much as to say,

``Are you all like this?'' (She)
And two little old ladies, who were sitting further up the table, with shawls hanging over the backs of the chairs, looked back, clearly indicating
``We are not; we are genteel.'' (ladies)

``Eat your dinner, dear,'' (she)
she (Bartlett) said to Lucy, and began to toy again with the meat that she (Lucy) had once censured.

Lucy mumbled that those seemed very odd people opposite.

``Eat your dinner, dear. This pension is a failure. To-morrow we will make a change.'' (she)

Hardly had she (Lucy) announced this fell decision when she (Lucy) reversed it. The curtains at the end of the room parted, and revealed a clergyman, stout but attractive, who hurried forward to take his (clergyman) place at the table, cheerfully apologizing for his (clergyman) lateness. Lucy, who had not yet acquired decency, at once rose to her (Lucy) feet, exclaiming:

``Oh, oh! Why, it's Mr. Beebe! Oh, how perfectly lovely! Oh, Charlotte, we must stop now, however bad the rooms are. Oh!'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)

Miss Bartlett said, with more restraint:

``How do you do, Mr. Beebe? I expect that you have forgotten us: Miss Bartlett and Miss Honeychurch, who were at Tunbridge Wells when you helped the Vicar of St. Peter's that very cold Easter.'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

The clergyman, who had the air of one on a holiday, did not remember the ladies quite as clearly as they remembered him (clergyman). But he (clergyman) came forward pleasantly enough and accepted the chair into which he (clergyman) was beckoned by Lucy.

``I AM so glad to see you,'' (the girl)
said the girl, who was in a state of spiritual starvation, and would have been glad to see the waiter if her (Lucy) cousin had permitted it.
``Just fancy how small the world is. Summer Street, too, makes it so specially funny.'' (unknown)

``Miss Honeychurch lives in the parish of Summer Street,'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)
said Miss Bartlett, filling up the gap,
``and she (Honeychurch) happened to tell me in the course of conversation that you have just accepted the living--'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

``Yes, I heard from mother so last week. She (mother) didn't know that I knew you at Tunbridge Wells; but I wrote back at once, and I said: `Mr. Beebe is--'''

``Quite right,'' (the clergyman)
said the clergyman.
``I move into the Rectory at Summer Street next June. I am lucky to be appointed to such a charming neighbourhood.'' (unknown)

``Oh, how glad I am! The name of our house is Windy Corner.'' (Mr. Beebe/Beebe)
Mr. Beebe bowed.

``There is mother and me generally, and my brother, though it's not often we get him (brother) to ch-- The church is rather far off, I mean.'' (unknown)

``Lucy, dearest, let Mr. Beebe eat his (Beebe) dinner.'' (unknown)

``I am eating it, thank you, and enjoying it.'' (He)

He (Beebe) preferred to talk to Lucy, whose playing he (Beebe) remembered, rather than to Miss Bartlett, who probably remembered his (Beebe) sermons. He (Beebe) asked the girl whether she (girl) knew Florence well, and was informed at some length that she (girl) had never been there before. It is delightful to advise a newcomer, and he (newcomer) was first in the field.

``Don't neglect the country round,'' (unknown)
his (newcomer) advice concluded.
``The first fine afternoon drive up to Fiesole, and round by Settignano, or something of that sort.'' (unknown)

``No!'' (unknown)
cried a voice from the top of the table.
``Mr. Beebe, you are wrong. The first fine afternoon your ladies must go to Prato.'' (unknown)

``That lady looks so clever,'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)
whispered Miss Bartlett to her (Bartlett) cousin.
``We are in luck.'' (unknown)

And, indeed, a perfect torrent of information burst on them. People told them what to see, when to see it, how to stop the electric trams, how to get rid of the beggars, how much to give for a vellum blotter, how much the place would grow upon them. The Pension Bertolini had decided, almost enthusiastically, that they would do. Whichever way they looked, kind ladies smiled and shouted at them. And above all rose the voice of the clever lady, crying:

``Prato! They must go to Prato. That place is too sweetly squalid for words. I love it; I revel in shaking off the trammels of respectability, as you know.'' (the lady)

The young man named George glanced at the clever lady, and then returned moodily to his (George) plate. Obviously he (George) and his (George) father did not do. Lucy, in the midst of her (Lucy) success, found time to wish they did. It gave her (Lucy) no extra pleasure that any one should be left in the cold; and when she (Lucy) rose to go, she (Lucy) turned back and gave the two outsiders a nervous little bow.

The father did not see it; the son acknowledged it, not by another bow, but by raising his (son) eyebrows and smiling; he (son) seemed to be smiling across something.

She (Lucy) hastened after her (Lucy) cousin, who had already disappeared through the curtains--curtains which smote one in the face, and seemed heavy with more than cloth. Beyond them stood the unreliable Signora, bowing good-evening to her (Lucy) guests, and supported by `Enery, her (Lucy) little boy, and Victorier, her (Lucy) daughter. It made a curious little scene, this attempt of the Cockney to convey the grace and geniality of the South. And even more curious was the drawing-room, which attempted to rival the solid comfort of a Bloomsbury boarding-house. Was this really Italy?

Miss Bartlett was already seated on a tightly stuffed arm-chair, which had the colour and the contours of a tomato. She (Bartlett) was talking to Mr. Beebe, and as she (Bartlett) spoke, her (Bartlett) long narrow head drove backwards and forwards, slowly, regularly, as though she (Bartlett) were demolishing some invisible obstacle.

``We are most grateful to you,'' (she)
she (Bartlett) was saying.
``The first evening means so much. When you arrived we were in for a peculiarly mauvais quart d’heure.'' (He)

He (Beebe) expressed his (Beebe) regret.

``Do you, by any chance, know the name of an old man who sat opposite us at dinner?'' (unknown)

``Emerson.'' (unknown)

``Is he (Emerson) a friend of yours?'' (unknown)

``We are friendly--as one is in pensions.'' (unknown)

``Then I will say no more.'' (He)

He (Beebe) pressed her (Bartlett) very slightly, and she (Bartlett) said more.

``I am, as it were,'' (she)
she (Bartlett) concluded,
``the chaperon of my young cousin, Lucy, and it would be a serious thing if I put her (Lucy) under an obligation to people of whom we know nothing. His (cousin) manner was somewhat unfortunate. I hope I acted for the best.'' (she)

``You acted very naturally,'' (he)
said he (Beebe). He (Beebe) seemed thoughtful, and after a few moments added:
``All the same, I don't think much harm would have come of accepting.'' (He)

``No harm, of course. But we could not be under an obligation.'' (unknown)

``He (cousin) is rather a peculiar man.'' (he)
Again he (Beebe) hesitated, and then said gently:
``I think he (cousin) would not take advantage of your acceptance, nor expect you to show gratitude. He (cousin) has the merit--if it is one--of saying exactly what he (cousin) means. He (cousin) has rooms he (cousin) does not value, and he (cousin) thinks you would value them. He (cousin) no more thought of putting you under an obligation than he (cousin) thought of being polite. It is so difficult--at least, I find it difficult--to understand people who speak the truth.'' (he)

Lucy was pleased, and said:

``I was hoping that he (cousin) was nice; I do so always hope that people will be nice.'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)

``I think he (cousin) is; nice and tiresome. I differ from him (cousin) on almost every point of any importance, and so, I expect--I may say I hope--you will differ. But his (cousin) is a type one disagrees with rather than deplores. When he (cousin) first came here he (cousin) not unnaturally put people's backs up. He (cousin) has no tact and no manners--I don't mean by that that he (cousin) has bad manners--and he (cousin) will not keep his (cousin) opinions to himself. We nearly complained about him (cousin) to our depressing Signora, but I am glad to say we thought better of it.'' (unknown)

``Am I to conclude,'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)
said Miss Bartlett,
``that he (cousin) is a Socialist?'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

Mr. Beebe accepted the convenient word, not without a slight twitching of the lips.

``And presumably he (cousin) has brought up his (cousin) son to be a Socialist, too?'' (unknown)

``I hardly know George, for he (George) hasn't learnt to talk yet. He (George) seems a nice creature, and I think he (George) has brains. Of course, he (George) has all his (George) father's mannerisms, and it is quite possible that he (George), too, may be a Socialist.'' (unknown)

``Oh, you relieve me,'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)
said Miss Bartlett.
``So you think I ought to have accepted their offer? You feel I have been narrow-minded and suspicious?'' (unknown)

``Not at all,'' (he)
he (Beebe) answered;
``I never suggested that.'' (he)

``But ought I not to apologize, at all events, for my apparent rudeness?'' (He)

He (Beebe) replied, with some irritation, that it would be quite unnecessary, and got up from his (Beebe) seat to go to the smoking-room.

``Was I a bore?'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)
said Miss Bartlett, as soon as he (Beebe) had disappeared.
``Why didn't you talk, Lucy? He (Socialist) prefers young people, I'm sure. I do hope I haven't monopolized him (Socialist). I hoped you would have him (Socialist) all the evening, as well as all dinner-time.'' (unknown)

``He (Socialist) is nice,'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)
exclaimed Lucy.
``Just what I remember. He (Socialist) seems to see good in everyone. No one would take him (Socialist) for a clergyman.'' (unknown)

``My dear Lucia--'' (unknown)

``Well, you know what I mean. And you know how clergymen generally laugh; Mr. Beebe laughs just like an ordinary man.'' (unknown)

``Funny girl! How you do remind me of your mother. I wonder if she (mother) will approve of Mr. Beebe.'' (unknown)

``I'm sure she (mother) will; and so will Freddy.'' (unknown)

``I think everyone at Windy Corner will approve; it is the fashionable world. I am used to Tunbridge Wells, where we are all hopelessly behind the times.'' (unknown)

``Yes,'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)
said Lucy despondently.

There was a haze of disapproval in the air, but whether the disapproval was of herself, or of Mr. Beebe, or of the fashionable world at Windy Corner, or of the narrow world at Tunbridge Wells, she (Windy) could not determine. She (Windy) tried to locate it, but as usual she (Windy) blundered. Miss Bartlett sedulously denied disapproving of any one, and added

``I am afraid you are finding me a very depressing companion.'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

And the girl again thought:

``I must have been selfish or unkind; I must be more careful. It is so dreadful for Charlotte, being poor.'' (the girl)

Fortunately one of the little old ladies, who for some time had been smiling very benignly, now approached and asked if she (girl) might be allowed to sit where Mr. Beebe had sat. Permission granted, she (girl) began to chatter gently about Italy, the plunge it had been to come there, the gratifying success of the plunge, the improvement in her (girl) sister's health, the necessity of closing the bed-room windows at night, and of thoroughly emptying the water-bottles in the morning. She (girl) handled her (girl) subjects agreeably, and they were, perhaps, more worthy of attention than the high discourse upon Guelfs and Ghibellines which was proceeding tempestuously at the other end of the room. It was a real catastrophe, not a mere episode, that evening of hers at Venice, when she (girl) had found in her (girl) bedroom something that is one worse than a flea, though one better than something else.

``But here you are as safe as in England. Signora Bertolini is so English.'' (unknown)

``Yet our rooms smell,'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)
said poor Lucy.
``We dread going to bed.'' (unknown)

``Ah, then you look into the court.'' (She)
She (Lucy) sighed.
``If only Mr. Emerson was more tactful! We were so sorry for you at dinner.'' (unknown)

``I think he (Emerson) was meaning to be kind.'' (unknown)

``Undoubtedly he (Emerson) was,'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

``Mr. Beebe has just been scolding me for my suspicious nature. Of course, I was holding back on my cousin's account.'' (unknown)

``Of course,'' (the lady)
said the little old lady; and they murmured that one could not be too careful with a young girl.

Lucy tried to look demure, but could not help feeling a great fool. No one was careful with her (Lucy) at home; or, at all events, she (Lucy) had not noticed it.

``About old Mr. Emerson--I hardly know. No, he (Emerson) is not tactful; yet, have you ever noticed that there are people who do things which are most indelicate, and yet at the same time--beautiful?'' (unknown)

``Beautiful?'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

``So one would have thought,'' (unknown)

``But things are so difficult, I sometimes think.'' (She)

``Miss Bartlett,'' (he)
he (Beebe) cried,
``it's all right about the rooms. I'm so glad. Mr. Emerson was talking about it in the smoking-room, and knowing what I did, I encouraged him (Emerson) to make the offer again. He (Emerson) has let me come and ask you. He (Emerson) would be so pleased.'' (he)

``Oh, Charlotte,'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)

``we must have the rooms now. The old man is just as nice and kind as he (man) can be.'' (cousin)

Miss Bartlett was silent.

``I fear,'' (Mr. Beebe/Beebe)

``My own wishes, dearest Lucy, are unimportant in comparison with yours. It would be hard indeed if I stopped you doing as you liked at Florence, when I am only here through your kindness. If you wish me to turn these gentlemen out of their rooms, I will do it. Would you then, Mr. Beebe, kindly tell Mr. Emerson that I accept his (Beebe) kind offer, and then conduct him (Beebe) to me, in order that I may thank him (Beebe) personally?'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

She (Bartlett) raised her (Bartlett) voice as she (Bartlett) spoke; it was heard all over the drawing-room, and silenced the Guelfs and the Ghibellines. The clergyman, inwardly cursing the female sex, bowed, and departed with her (Bartlett) message.

``Remember, Lucy, I alone am implicated in this. I do not wish the acceptance to come from you. Grant me that, at all events.'' (Mr. Beebe/Beebe)

Mr. Beebe was back, saying rather nervously:

``Mr. Emerson is engaged, but here is his (Emerson) son instead.'' (Mr. Beebe/Beebe)

The young man gazed down on the three ladies, who felt seated on the floor, so low were their chairs.

``My father,'' (he)
he (Beebe) said,
``is in his (Emerson) bath, so you cannot thank him (Emerson) personally. But any message given by you to me will be given by me to him (Emerson) as soon as he (Emerson) comes out.'' (he)

Miss Bartlett was unequal to the bath. All her (Bartlett) barbed civilities came forth wrong end first. Young Mr. Emerson scored a notable triumph to the delight of Mr. Beebe and to the secret delight of Lucy.

``Poor young man!'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

``Oh, dear!'' (the lady)
breathed the little old lady, and shuddered as if all the winds of heaven had entered the apartment.
``Gentlemen sometimes do not realize--'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)
Her (lady) voice faded away, but Miss Bartlett seemed to understand and a conversation developed, in which gentlemen who did not thoroughly realize played a principal part. Lucy, not realizing either, was reduced to literature. Taking up Baedeker's Handbook to Northern Italy, she (Lucy) committed to memory the most important dates of Florentine History. For she (Lucy) was determined to enjoy herself on the morrow. Thus the half-hour crept profitably away, and at last Miss Bartlett rose with a sigh, and said:

``I think one might venture now. No, Lucy, do not stir. I will superintend the move.'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

``How you do do everything,'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)
said Lucy.

``Naturally, dear. It is my affair.'' (unknown)

``But I would like to help you.'' (unknown)

``No, dear.'' (Charlotte)

Charlotte's energy! And her (Lucy) unselfishness! She (Lucy) had been thus all her (Lucy) life, but really, on this Italian tour, she (Lucy) was surpassing herself. So Lucy felt, or strove to feel. And yet--there was a rebellious spirit in her (Lucy) which wondered whether the acceptance might not have been less delicate and more beautiful. At all events, she (Lucy) entered her (Lucy) own room without any feeling of joy.

``I want to explain,'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)
said Miss Bartlett,
``why it is that I have taken the largest room. Naturally, of course, I should have given it to you; but I happen to know that it belongs to the young man, and I was sure your mother would not like it.'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

Lucy was bewildered.

``If you are to accept a favour it is more suitable you should be under an obligation to his (man) father than to him (man). I am a woman of the world, in my small way, and I know where things lead to. However, Mr. Beebe is a guarantee of a sort that they will not presume on this.'' (unknown)

``Mother wouldn't mind I'm sure,'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)
said Lucy, but again had the sense of larger and unsuspected issues.

Miss Bartlett only sighed, and enveloped her (Bartlett) in a protecting embrace as she (Bartlett) wished her (Bartlett) good-night. It gave Lucy the sensation of a fog, and when she (Lucy) reached her (Lucy) own room she (Lucy) opened the window and breathed the clean night air, thinking of the kind old man who had enabled her (Lucy) to see the lights dancing in the Arno and the cypresses of San Miniato, and the foot-hills of the Apennines, black against the rising moon.

``What does it mean?'' (she)
she (Bartlett) thought, and she (Bartlett) examined it carefully by the light of a candle. Meaningless at first, it gradually became menacing, obnoxious, portentous with evil. Chapter II: In Santa Croce with No Baedeker

It was pleasant to wake up in Florence, to open the eyes upon a bright bare room, with a floor of red tiles which look clean though they are not; with a painted ceiling whereon pink griffins and blue amorini sport in a forest of yellow violins and bassoons. It was pleasant, too, to fling wide the windows, pinching the fingers in unfamiliar fastenings, to lean out into sunshine with beautiful hills and trees and marble churches opposite, and close below, the Arno, gurgling against the embankment of the road.

Over the river men were at work with spades and sieves on the sandy foreshore, and on the river was a boat, also diligently employed for some mysterious end. An electric tram came rushing underneath the window. No one was inside it, except one tourist; but its platforms were overflowing with Italians, who preferred to stand. Children tried to hang on behind, and the conductor, with no malice, spat in their faces to make them let go. Then soldiers appeared--good-looking, undersized men--wearing each a knapsack covered with mangy fur, and a great-coat which had been cut for some larger soldier. Beside them walked officers, looking foolish and fierce, and before them went little boys, turning somersaults in time with the band. The tramcar became entangled in their ranks, and moved on painfully, like a caterpillar in a swarm of ants. One of the little boys fell down, and some white bullocks came out of an archway. Indeed, if it had not been for the good advice of an old man who was selling button-hooks, the road might never have got clear.

Over such trivialities as these many a valuable hour may slip away, and the traveller who has gone to Italy to study the tactile values of Giotto, or the corruption of the Papacy, may return remembering nothing but the blue sky and the men and women who live under it. So it was as well that Miss Bartlett should tap and come in, and having commented on Lucy's leaving the door unlocked, and on her (Lucy) leaning out of the window before she (Lucy) was fully dressed, should urge her (Lucy) to hasten herself, or the best of the day would be gone. By the time Lucy was ready her (Lucy) cousin had done her (Lucy) breakfast, and was listening to the clever lady among the crumbs.

A conversation then ensued, on not unfamiliar lines. Miss Bartlett was, after all, a wee bit tired, and thought they had better spend the morning settling in; unless Lucy would at all like to go out? Lucy would rather like to go out, as it was her (Lucy) first day in Florence, but, of course, she (Lucy) could go alone. Miss Bartlett could not allow this. Of course she (Bartlett) would accompany Lucy everywhere. Oh, certainly not; Lucy would stop with her (Lucy) cousin. Oh, no! that would never do. Oh, yes!

At this point the clever lady broke in.

``If it is Mrs. Grundy who is troubling you, I do assure you that you can neglect the good person. Being English, Miss Honeychurch will be perfectly safe. Italians understand. A dear friend of mine, Contessa Baroncelli, has two daughters, and when she (Honeychurch) cannot send a maid to school with them, she (Honeychurch) lets them go in sailor-hats instead. Every one takes them for English, you see, especially if their hair is strained tightly behind.'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)

Miss Bartlett was unconvinced by the safety of Contessa Baroncelli's daughters. She (Bartlett) was determined to take Lucy herself, her (Lucy) head not being so very bad. The clever lady then said that she (lady) was going to spend a long morning in Santa Croce, and if Lucy would come too, she (Lucy) would be delighted.

``I will take you by a dear dirty back way, Miss Honeychurch, and if you bring me luck, we shall have an adventure.'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)

Lucy said that this was most kind, and at once opened the Baedeker, to see where Santa Croce was.

``Tut, tut! Miss Lucy! I hope we shall soon emancipate you from Baedeker. He (Baroncelli) does but touch the surface of things. As to the true Italy--he (Baroncelli) does not even dream of it. The true Italy is only to be found by patient observation.'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)

This sounded very interesting, and Lucy hurried over her (Lucy) breakfast, and started with her (Lucy) new friend in high spirits. Italy was coming at last. The Cockney Signora and her (Lucy) works had vanished like a bad dream.

Miss Lavish--for that was the clever lady's name--turned to the right along the sunny Lung' Arno. How delightfully warm! But a wind down the side streets cut like a knife, didn't it? Ponte alle Grazie--particularly interesting, mentioned by Dante. San Miniato--beautiful as well as interesting; the crucifix that kissed a murderer--Miss Honeychurch would remember the story. Accordingly they drifted through a series of those grey-brown streets, neither commodious nor picturesque, in which the eastern quarter of the city abounds. Lucy soon lost interest in the discontent of Lady Louisa, and became discontented herself. For one ravishing moment Italy appeared. She (Lucy) stood in the Square of the Annunziata and saw in the living terra-cotta those divine babies whom no cheap reproduction can ever stale. There they stood, with their shining limbs bursting from the garments of charity, and their strong white arms extended against circlets of heaven. The hour was approaching at which the continental breakfast begins, or rather ceases, to tell, and the ladies bought some hot chestnut paste out of a little shop, because it looked so typical. It tasted partly of the paper in which it was wrapped, partly of hair oil, partly of the great unknown. But it gave them strength to drift into another Piazza, large and dusty, on the farther side of which rose a black-and-white facade of surpassing ugliness. Miss Lavish spoke to it dramatically. It was Santa Croce. The adventure was over.

Then the pernicious charm of Italy worked on her (Lucy), and, instead of acquiring information, she (Lucy) began to be happy. She (Lucy) beheld the horrible fate that overtook three Papists--two he-babies and a she-baby--who began their career by sousing each other with the Holy Water, and then proceeded to the Machiavelli memorial, dripping but hallowed. ```From far, from eve and morning, And yon twelve-winded sky, The stuff of life to knit me Blew hither: here am I'

George and I both know this, but why does it distress him (George)? We know that we come from the winds, and that we shall return to them; that all life is perhaps a knot, a tangle, a blemish in the eternal smoothness. But why should this make us unhappy? Let us rather love one another, and work and rejoice. I don't believe in this world sorrow.''

Miss Honeychurch assented.

``Then make my boy think like us. Make him (boy) realize that by the side of the everlasting Why there is a Yes--a transitory Yes if you like, but a Yes.'' (she)

Suddenly she (Honeychurch) laughed; surely one ought to laugh. A young man melancholy because the universe wouldn't fit, because life was a tangle or a wind, or a Yes, or something!

``I'm very sorry,'' (she)
she (Honeychurch) cried.
``You'll think me unfeeling, but--but--'' (she)

Chapter III: Music, Violets, and the Letter

``S'' (Violets)

It so happened that Lucy, who found daily life rather chaotic, entered a more solid world when she (Lucy) opened the piano. She (Lucy) was then no longer either deferential or patronizing; no longer either a rebel or a slave. The kingdom of music is not the kingdom of this world; it will accept those whom breeding and intellect and culture have alike rejected. The commonplace person begins to play, and shoots into the empyrean without effort, whilst we look up, marvelling how he (slave) has escaped us, and thinking how we could worship him (slave) and love him (slave), would he (slave) but translate his (slave) visions into human words, and his (slave) experiences into human actions. Perhaps he (slave) cannot; certainly he (slave) does not, or does so very seldom. Lucy had done so never.

She (Lucy) was no dazzling executante; her (Lucy) runs were not at all like strings of pearls, and she (Lucy) struck no more right notes than was suitable for one of her (Lucy) age and situation. Nor was she (Lucy) the passionate young lady, who performs so tragically on a summer's evening with the window open. Passion was there, but it could not be easily labelled; it slipped between love and hatred and jealousy, and all the furniture of the pictorial style. And she (Lucy) was tragical only in the sense that she (Lucy) was great, for she (Lucy) loved to play on the side of Victory. Victory of what and over what--that is more than the words of daily life can tell us. But that some sonatas of Beethoven are written tragic no one can gainsay; yet they can triumph or despair as the player decides, and Lucy had decided that they should triumph.

A very wet afternoon at the Bertolini permitted her (Lucy) to do the thing she (Lucy) really liked, and after lunch she (Lucy) opened the little draped piano. A few people lingered round and praised her (Lucy) playing, but finding that she (Lucy) made no reply, dispersed to their rooms to write up their diaries or to sleep. She (Lucy) took no notice of Mr. Emerson looking for his (Emerson) son, nor of Miss Bartlett looking for Miss Lavish, nor of Miss Lavish looking for her (Bartlett) cigarette-case. Like every true performer, she (Bartlett) was intoxicated by the mere feel of the notes: they were fingers caressing her (Bartlett) own; and by touch, not by sound alone, did she (Bartlett) come to her (Bartlett) desire.

It was at one of those entertainments where the upper classes entertain the lower. ``Miss Honeychurch. Piano. Beethoven,'' (Mr. Beebe/Beebe)

Chapter IV: Fourth Chapter

Mr. Beebe was right. Lucy never knew her (Lucy) desires so clearly as after music. She (Lucy) had not really appreciated the clergyman's wit, nor the suggestive twitterings of Miss Alan. Conversation was tedious; she (Alan) wanted something big, and she (Alan) believed that it would have come to her (Alan) on the wind-swept platform of an electric tram. This she (Alan) might not attempt. It was unladylike. Why? Why were most big things unladylike? Charlotte had once explained to her (Charlotte) why. It was not that ladies were inferior to men; it was that they were different. Their mission was to inspire others to achievement rather than to achieve themselves. Indirectly, by means of tact and a spotless name, a lady could accomplish much. But if she (lady) rushed into the fray herself she (lady) would be first censured, then despised, and finally ignored. Poems had been written to illustrate this point.

There is much that is immortal in this medieval lady. The dragons have gone, and so have the knights, but still she (lady) lingers in our midst. She (lady) reigned in many an early Victorian castle, and was Queen of much early Victorian song. It is sweet to protect her (Queen) in the intervals of business, sweet to pay her (Queen) honour when she (Queen) has cooked our dinner well. But alas! the creature grows degenerate. In her (Queen) heart also there are springing up strange desires. She (Queen) too is enamoured of heavy winds, and vast panoramas, and green expanses of the sea. She (Queen) has marked the kingdom of this world, how full it is of wealth, and beauty, and war--a radiant crust, built around the central fires, spinning towards the receding heavens. Men, declaring that she (Queen) inspires them to it, move joyfully over the surface, having the most delightful meetings with other men, happy, not because they are masculine, but because they are alive. Before the show breaks up she (Queen) would like to drop the august title of the Eternal Woman, and go there as her (Woman) transitory self.

Lucy does not stand for the medieval lady, who was rather an ideal to which she (Lucy) was bidden to lift her (Lucy) eyes when feeling serious. Nor has she (Lucy) any system of revolt. Here and there a restriction annoyed her (Lucy) particularly, and she (Lucy) would transgress it, and perhaps be sorry that she (Lucy) had done so. This afternoon she (Lucy) was peculiarly restive. She (Lucy) would really like to do something of which her (Lucy) well-wishers disapproved. As she (Lucy) might not go on the electric tram, she (Lucy) went to Alinari's shop.

There she (Lucy) bought a photograph of Botticelli's

``Birth of Venus.'' (Botticelli)

``The world,'' (she)

``Nothing ever happens to me,'' (she)
she (Honeychurch) reflected, as she (Honeychurch) entered the Piazza Signoria and looked nonchalantly at its marvels, now fairly familiar to her (Honeychurch). The great square was in shadow; the sunshine had come too late to strike it. Neptune was already unsubstantial in the twilight, half god, half ghost, and his (god) fountain plashed dreamily to the men and satyrs who idled together on its marge. The Loggia showed as the triple entrance of a cave, wherein many a deity, shadowy, but immortal, looking forth upon the arrivals and departures of mankind. It was the hour of unreality--the hour, that is, when unfamiliar things are real. An older person at such an hour and in such a place might think that sufficient was happening to him (deity), and rest content. Lucy desired more.

He (Eager) knew the people who never walked about with Baedekers, who had learnt to take a siesta after lunch, who took drives the pension tourists had never heard of, and saw by private influence galleries which were closed to them. The Piazza Signoria is too stony to be brilliant. It has no grass, no flowers, no frescoes, no glittering walls of marble or comforting patches of ruddy brick. By an odd chance--unless we believe in a presiding genius of places--the statues that relieve its severity suggest, not the innocence of childhood, nor the glorious bewilderment of youth, but the conscious achievements of maturity. Perseus and Judith, Hercules and Thusnelda, they have done or suffered something, and though they are immortal, immortality has come to them after experience, not before. Chapter VII: They Return

Some complicated game had been playing up and down the hillside all the afternoon. What it was and exactly how the players had sided, Lucy was slow to discover. Mr. Eager had met them with a questioning eye. Charlotte had repulsed him (Eager) with much small talk. Mr. Emerson, seeking his (Emerson) son, was told whereabouts to find him (Emerson). Pan had been amongst them--not the great god Pan, who has been buried these two thousand years, but the little god Pan, who presides over social contretemps and unsuccessful picnics. Part Two

Chapter VIII: Medieval

The drawing-room curtains at Windy Corner had been pulled to meet, for the carpet was new and deserved protection from the August sun. They were heavy curtains, reaching almost to the ground, and the light that filtered through them was subdued and varied. A poet--none was present--might have quoted,

``Life like a dome of many coloured glass,'' (A poet)
or might have compared the curtains to sluice-gates, lowered against the intolerable tides of heaven. Without was poured a sea of radiance; within, the glory, though visible, was tempered to the capacities of man.

Two pleasant people sat in the room. One--a boy of nineteen--was studying a small manual of anatomy, and peering occasionally at a bone which lay upon the piano. From time to time he (boy) bounced in his (boy) chair and puffed and groaned, for the day was hot and the print small, and the human frame fearfully made; and his (boy) mother, who was writing a letter, did continually read out to him (boy) what she (mother) had written. said the boy, who was Freddy, Lucy's brother.

Well educated, well endowed, and not deficient physically, he (Cecil) remained in the grip of a certain devil whom the modern world knows as self-consciousness, and whom the medieval, with dimmer vision, worshipped as asceticism. A Gothic statue implies celibacy, just as a Greek statue implies fruition, and perhaps this was what Mr. Beebe meant. ``story.'' (Leonardo)

He (Freddy) took his (Freddy) tone from her (lady). There was no more heavy beneficence, no more attempts to dignify the situation with poetry or the Scriptures. None of them dared or was able to be serious any more.

An engagement is so potent a thing that sooner or later it reduces all who speak of it to this state of cheerful awe. Away from it, in the solitude of their rooms, Mr. Beebe, and even Freddy, might again be critical. But in its presence and in the presence of each other they were sincerely hilarious. It has a strange power, for it compels not only the lips, but the very heart. The chief parallel to compare one great thing with another--is the power over us of a temple of some alien creed. Standing outside, we deride or oppose it, or at the most feel sentimental. Inside, though the saints and gods are not ours, we become true believers, in case any true believer should be present.

So it was that after the gropings and the misgivings of the afternoon they pulled themselves together and settled down to a very pleasant tea-party. If they were hypocrites they did not know it, and their hypocrisy had every chance of setting and of becoming true. Anne, putting down each plate as if it were a wedding present, stimulated them greatly. They could not lag behind that smile of hers which she (Anne) gave them ere she (Anne) kicked the drawing-room door. Mr. Beebe chirruped. Freddy was at his (Freddy) wittiest, referring to Cecil as the

``Fiasco'' (Cecil)
--family honoured pun on fiance. Mrs. Honeychurch, amusing and portly, promised well as a mother-in-law. As for Lucy and Cecil, for whom the temple had been built, they also joined in the merry ritual, but waited, as earnest worshippers should, for the disclosure of some holier shrine of joy.

Chapter IX: Lucy As a Work of Art

A few days after the engagement was announced Mrs. Honeychurch made Lucy and her (Lucy) Fiasco come to a little garden-party in the neighbourhood, for naturally she (Lucy) wanted to show people that her (Lucy) daughter was marrying a presentable man.

Cecil was more than presentable; he (Cecil) looked distinguished, and it was very pleasant to see his (Cecil) slim figure keeping step with Lucy, and his (Cecil) long, fair face responding when Lucy spoke to him (Cecil). People congratulated Mrs. Honeychurch, which is, I believe, a social blunder, but it pleased her (Lucy), and she (Lucy) introduced Cecil rather indiscriminately to some stuffy dowagers.

At tea a misfortune took place: a cup of coffee was upset over Lucy's figured silk, and though Lucy feigned indifference, her (Lucy) mother feigned nothing of the sort but dragged her (Lucy) indoors to have the frock treated by a sympathetic maid. They were gone some time, and Cecil was left with the dowagers. When they returned he (Cecil) was not as pleasant as he (Cecil) had been.

``Do you go to much of this sort of thing?'' (he)
he (Cecil) asked when they were driving home.

``Oh, now and then,'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)
said Lucy, who had rather enjoyed herself.

``Is it typical of country society?'' (unknown)

``I suppose so. Mother, would it be?'' (unknown)

``Plenty of society,'' (Mrs. Honeychurch)
said Mrs. Honeychurch, who was trying to remember the hang of one of the dresses.

``To me it seemed perfectly appalling, disastrous, portentous.'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)

``I am so sorry that you were stranded.'' (unknown)

``One has to go through it, I suppose. They won't notice us so much next time.'' (unknown)

``But my point is that their whole attitude is wrong. An engagement--horrid word in the first place--is a private matter, and should be treated as such.'' (the women)

Yet the smirking old women, however wrong individually, were racially correct. The spirit of the generations had smiled through them, rejoicing in the engagement of Cecil and Lucy because it promised the continuance of life on earth. To Cecil and Lucy it promised something quite different--personal love. Hence Cecil's irritation and Lucy's belief that his (Cecil) irritation was just.

``How tiresome!'' (she)
she (Lucy) said.
``Couldn't you have escaped to tennis?'' (unknown)

``Inglese Italianato?'' (unknown)

``E un diavolo incarnato! You know the proverb?'' (She)

She (Lucy) did not. Nor did it seem applicable to a young man who had spent a quiet winter in Rome with his (man) mother. But Cecil, since his (Cecil) engagement, had taken to affect a cosmopolitan naughtiness which he (Cecil) was far from possessing.

``Well,'' (he)
said he (Cecil),

Chapter X: Cecil as a Humourist

The society out of which Cecil proposed to rescue Lucy was perhaps no very splendid affair, yet it was more splendid than her (Lucy) antecedents entitled her (Lucy) to. Her (Lucy) father, a prosperous local solicitor, had built Windy Corner, as a speculation at the time the district was opening up, and, falling in love with his (father) own creation, had ended by living there himself. Soon after his (father) marriage the social atmosphere began to alter. Other houses were built on the brow of that steep southern slope and others, again, among the pine-trees behind, and northward on the chalk barrier of the downs. Most of these houses were larger than Windy Corner, and were filled by people who came, not from the district, but from London, and who mistook the Honeychurches for the remnants of an indigenous aristocracy. He (father) was inclined to be frightened, but his (father) wife accepted the situation without either pride or humility.

``I cannot think what people are doing,'' (she)
she (wife) would say,
``but it is extremely fortunate for the children.'' (she)
She (wife) called everywhere; her (wife) calls were returned with enthusiasm, and by the time people found out that she (wife) was not exactly of their milieu, they liked her (wife), and it did not seem to matter. When Mr. Honeychurch died, he (Honeychurch) had the satisfaction--which few honest solicitors despise--of leaving his (Honeychurch) family rooted in the best society obtainable.

The best obtainable. Certainly many of the immigrants were rather dull, and Lucy realized this more vividly since her (Lucy) return from Italy. Hitherto she (Lucy) had accepted their ideals without questioning--their kindly affluence, their inexplosive religion, their dislike of paper-bags, orange-peel, and broken bottles. A Radical out and out, she (Lucy) learnt to speak with horror of Suburbia. Life, so far as she (Lucy) troubled to conceive it, was a circle of rich, pleasant people, with identical interests and identical foes. In this circle, one thought, married, and died. Outside it were poverty and vulgarity for ever trying to enter, just as the London fog tries to enter the pine-woods pouring through the gaps in the northern hills. But, in Italy, where any one who chooses may warm himself in equality, as in the sun, this conception of life vanished. Her (Lucy) senses expanded; she (Lucy) felt that there was no one whom she (Lucy) might not get to like, that social barriers were irremovable, doubtless, but not particularly high. You jump over them just as you jump into a peasant's olive-yard in the Apennines, and he (peasant) is glad to see you. She (Lucy) returned with new eyes.

So did Cecil; but Italy had quickened Cecil, not to tolerance, but to irritation. He (Cecil) saw that the local society was narrow, but, instead of saying,

``Does that very much matter?'' (He)
he (Cecil) rebelled, and tried to substitute for it the society he (Cecil) called broad. He (Cecil) did not realize that Lucy had consecrated her (Lucy) environment by the thousand little civilities that create a tenderness in time, and that though her (Lucy) eyes saw its defects, her (Lucy) heart refused to despise it entirely. Nor did he (Cecil) realize a more important point--that if she (Lucy) was too great for this society, she (Lucy) was too great for all society, and had reached the stage where personal intercourse would alone satisfy her (Lucy). A rebel she (Lucy) was, but not of the kind he (rebel) understood--a rebel who desired, not a wider dwelling-room, but equality beside the man she (Lucy) loved. For Italy was offering her (Lucy) the most priceless of all possessions--her (Lucy) own soul.

Playing bumble-puppy with Minnie Beebe, niece to the rector, and aged thirteen--an ancient and most honourable game, which consists in striking tennis-balls high into the air, so that they fall over the net and immoderately bounce; some hit Mrs. Honeychurch; others are lost. Chapter XI: In Mrs. Vyse's Well-Appointed Flat

The Comic Muse, though able to look after her (Vyse) own interests, did not disdain the assistance of Mr. Vyse. His (Vyse) idea of bringing the Emersons to Windy Corner struck her (Windy) as decidedly good, and she (Windy) carried through the negotiations without a hitch. Sir Harry Otway signed the agreement, met Mr. Emerson, who was duly disillusioned. The Miss Alans were duly offended, and wrote a dignified letter to Lucy, whom they held responsible for the failure. Mr. Beebe planned pleasant moments for the new-comers, and told Mrs. Honeychurch that Freddy must call on them as soon as they arrived. Indeed, so ample was the Muse's equipment that she (Honeychurch) permitted Mr. Harris, never a very robust criminal, to droop his (Harris) head, to be forgotten, and to die.

Lucy--to descend from bright heaven to earth, whereon there are shadows because there are hills--Lucy was at first plunged into despair, but settled after a little thought that it did not matter the very least. Now that she (Lucy) was engaged, the Emersons would scarcely insult her (Lucy) and were welcome into the neighbourhood. And Cecil was welcome to bring whom he (Cecil) would into the neighbourhood. Therefore Cecil was welcome to bring the Emersons into the neighbourhood. But, as I say, this took a little thinking, and--so illogical are girls--the event remained rather greater and rather more dreadful than it should have done. She (Lucy) was glad that a visit to Mrs. Vyse now fell due; the tenants moved into Cissie Villa while she (Vyse) was safe in the London flat.

``Cecil--Cecil darling,'' (she)
she (Vyse) whispered the evening she (Vyse) arrived, and crept into his (Cecil) arms.

``So you do love me, little thing?'' (he)
he (Cecil) murmured.

``Oh, Cecil, I do, I do! I don't know what I should do without you.'' (unknown)

Several days passed. Then she (Lucy) had a letter from Miss Bartlett. A coolness had sprung up between the two cousins, and they had not corresponded since they parted in August. The coolness dated from what Charlotte would call

``the flight to Rome,'' (Charlotte)
and in Rome it had increased amazingly. For the companion who is merely uncongenial in the mediaeval world becomes exasperating in the classical. Charlotte, unselfish in the Forum, would have tried a sweeter temper than Lucy's, and once, in the Baths of Caracalla, they had doubted whether they could continue their tour. Lucy had said she (Lucy) would join the Vyses--Mrs. Vyse was an acquaintance of her (Vyse) mother, so there was no impropriety in the plan and Miss Bartlett had replied that she (Bartlett) was quite used to being abandoned suddenly. Finally nothing happened; but the coolness remained, and, for Lucy, was even increased when she (Lucy) opened the letter and read as follows. It had been forwarded from Windy Corner.

``Tunbridge Wells,

``September.

``Dearest Lucia,

``I have news of you at last! Miss Lavish has been bicycling in your parts, but was not sure whether a call would be welcome. Puncturing her (I) tire near Summer Street, and it being mended while she (I) sat very woebegone in that pretty churchyard, she (I) saw to her (I) astonishment, a door open opposite and the younger Emerson man come out. He (Emerson) said his (Emerson) father had just taken the house. He (Emerson) SAID he (Emerson) did not know that you lived in the neighbourhood -LRB-?-RRB-. He (Emerson) never suggested giving Eleanor a cup of tea. Dear Lucy, I am much worried, and I advise you to make a clean breast of his (Emerson) past behaviour to your mother, Freddy, and Mr. Vyse, who will forbid him (Vyse) to enter the house, etc.. That was a great misfortune, and I dare say you have told them already. Mr. Vyse is so sensitive. I remember how I used to get on his (Vyse) nerves at Rome. I am very sorry about it all, and should not feel easy unless I warned you.

``Believe me,

``Your anxious and loving cousin,

``Charlotte.'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)

Lucy was much annoyed, and replied as follows:

``Beauchamp Mansions, S.W.

``Dear Charlotte,

``Many thanks for your warning. When Mr. Emerson forgot himself on the mountain, you made me promise not to tell mother, because you said she (mother) would blame you for not being always with me. I have kept that promise, and cannot possibly tell her (mother) now. I have said both to her (mother) and Cecil that I met the Emersons at Florence, and that they are respectable people--which I do think--and the reason that he (Cecil) offered Miss Lavish no tea was probably that he (Cecil) had none himself. She (mother) should have tried at the Rectory. I cannot begin making a fuss at this stage. You must see that it would be too absurd. If the Emersons heard I had complained of them, they would think themselves of importance, which is exactly what they are not. I like the old father, and look forward to seeing him (father) again. As for the son, I am sorry for him (son) when we meet, rather than for myself. They are known to Cecil, who is very well and spoke of you the other day. We expect to be married in January.

``Miss Lavish cannot have told you much about me, for I am not at Windy Corner at all, but here. Please do not put `Private' outside your envelope again. No one opens my letters.

``L. M. Honeychurch.'' (unknown)

Secrecy has this disadvantage: we lose the sense of proportion; we cannot tell whether our secret is important or not. Were Lucy and her (Lucy) cousin closeted with a great thing which would destroy Cecil's life if he (Cecil) discovered it, or with a little thing which he (Cecil) would laugh at? Miss Bartlett suggested the former. Perhaps she (Bartlett) was right. It had become a great thing now. Left to herself, Lucy would have told her (Lucy) mother and her (Lucy) lover ingenuously, and it would have remained a little thing.

``Emerson, not Harris'' (She)
; it was only that a few weeks ago. She (Lucy) tried to tell Cecil even now when they were laughing about some beautiful lady who had smitten his (Cecil) heart at school. But her (lady) body behaved so ridiculously that she (lady) stopped.

She (lady) and her (lady) secret stayed ten days longer in the deserted Metropolis visiting the scenes they were to know so well later on. It did her (lady) no harm, Cecil thought, to learn the framework of society, while society itself was absent on the golf-links or the moors. The weather was cool, and it did her (lady) no harm. In spite of the season, Mrs. Vyse managed to scrape together a dinner-party consisting entirely of the grandchildren of famous people. The food was poor, but the talk had a witty weariness that impressed the girl. One was tired of everything, it seemed. One launched into enthusiasms only to collapse gracefully, and pick oneself up amid sympathetic laughter. In this atmosphere the Pension Bertolini and Windy Corner appeared equally crude, and Lucy saw that her (Lucy) London career would estrange her (Lucy) a little from all that she (Lucy) had loved in the past.

The grandchildren asked her (Lucy) to play the piano.

She (Lucy) played Schumann.

``Now some Beethoven'' (Cecil)
called Cecil, when the querulous beauty of the music had died. She (Lucy) shook her (Lucy) head and played Schumann again. The melody rose, unprofitably magical. It broke; it was resumed broken, not marching once from the cradle to the grave. The sadness of the incomplete--the sadness that is often Life, but should never be Art--throbbed in its disjected phrases, and made the nerves of the audience throb. Not thus had she (Lucy) played on the little draped piano at the Bertolini, and
``Too much Schumann'' (Bertolini/Signora/Signora Bertolini)
was not the remark that Mr. Beebe had passed to himself when she (Lucy) returned.

The too vast orb of her (Vyse) fate had crushed her (Vyse); and she (Vyse) had seen too many seasons, too many cities, too many men, for her (Vyse) abilities, and even with Cecil she (Vyse) was mechanical, and behaved as if he (Cecil) was not one son, but, so to speak, a filial crowd.

Chapter XII: Twelfth Chapter

It was a Saturday afternoon, gay and brilliant after abundant rains, and the spirit of youth dwelt in it, though the season was now autumn. All that was gracious triumphed. As the motorcars passed through Summer Street they raised only a little dust, and their stench was soon dispersed by the wind and replaced by the scent of the wet birches or of the pines. Mr. Beebe, at leisure for life's amenities, leant over his (Beebe) Rectory gate. Freddy leant by him (Freddy), smoking a pendant pipe.

``Suppose we go and hinder those new people opposite for a little.'' (unknown)

``M'm.'' (unknown)

``They might amuse you.'' (Freddy)

Freddy, whom his (Freddy) fellow-creatures never amused, suggested that the new people might be feeling a bit busy, and so on, since they had only just moved in.

``I suggested we should hinder them,'' (Mr. Beebe/Beebe)

``Hullo!'' (unknown)

That evening and all that night the water ran away. On the morrow the pool had shrunk to its old size and lost its glory. It had been a call to the blood and to the relaxed will, a passing benediction whose influence did not pass, a holiness, a spell, a momentary chalice for youth.

Chapter XIII: How Miss Bartlett's Boiler Was So Tiresome

How often had Lucy rehearsed this bow, this interview! But she (Lucy) had always rehearsed them indoors, and with certain accessories, which surely we have a right to assume. Who could foretell that she (Lucy) and George would meet in the rout of a civilization, amidst an army of coats and collars and boots that lay wounded over the sunlit earth? She (Lucy) had imagined a young Mr. Emerson, who might be shy or morbid or indifferent or furtively impudent. She (Lucy) was prepared for all of these. But she (Lucy) had never imagined one who would be happy and greet her (Lucy) with the shout of the morning star.

Indoors herself, partaking of tea with old Mrs. Butterworth, she (Butterworth) reflected that it is impossible to foretell the future with any degree of accuracy, that it is impossible to rehearse life. A fault in the scenery, a face in the audience, an irruption of the audience on to the stage, and all our carefully planned gestures mean nothing, or mean too much.

``I will bow,'' (she)
she (Butterworth) had thought.
``I will not shake hands with him (Emerson). That will be just the proper thing.'' (She)
She (Butterworth) had bowed--but to whom? To gods, to heroes, to the nonsense of school-girls! She (Butterworth) had bowed across the rubbish that cumbers the world.

So ran her (Butterworth) thoughts, while her (Butterworth) faculties were busy with Cecil. It was another of those dreadful engagement calls. Mrs. Butterworth had wanted to see him (Cecil), and he (Cecil) did not want to be seen. He (Cecil) did not want to hear about hydrangeas, why they change their colour at the seaside. He (Cecil) did not want to join the C. O. S. When cross he (Cecil) was always elaborate, and made long, clever answers where

``Yes'' (he)
or
``No'' (he)
would have done. Lucy soothed him (Cecil) and tinkered at the conversation in a way that promised well for their married peace. No one is perfect, and surely it is wiser to discover the imperfections before wedlock. Miss Bartlett, indeed, though not in word, had taught the girl that this our life contains nothing satisfactory. Lucy, though she (Lucy) disliked the teacher, regarded the teaching as profound, and applied it to her (Lucy) lover.

``Lucy,'' (mother)
said her (Lucy) mother, when they got home,
``is anything the matter with Cecil?'' (mother)

The question was ominous; up till now Mrs. Honeychurch had behaved with charity and restraint.

``No, I don't think so, mother; Cecil's all right.'' (unknown)

So the grittiness went out of life. It generally did at Windy Corner. At the last minute, when the social machine was clogged hopelessly, one member or other of the family poured in a drop of oil. Cecil despised their methods--perhaps rightly. At all events, they were not his (Cecil) own.

``She (Lucy) was a novelist,'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)
said Lucy craftily. The remark was a happy one, for nothing roused Mrs. Honeychurch so much as literature in the hands of females. She (Honeychurch) would abandon every topic to inveigh against those women who -LRB-instead of minding their houses and their children-RRB- seek notoriety by print. Her (Honeychurch) attitude was:

Her (Bartlett) love to Lucy. And, equally of course, George Emerson could come to tennis on the Sunday week.

``things that came out of nothing and meant she (Lucy) didn't know what.'' (she)

It is obvious enough for the reader to conclude,

``She (Lucy) loves young Emerson.'' (the reader)

``nerves'' (She)

Chapter XV: The Disaster Within

The Sunday after Miss Bartlett's arrival was a glorious day, like most of the days of that year. In the Weald, autumn approached, breaking up the green monotony of summer, touching the parks with the grey bloom of mist, the beech-trees with russet, the oak-trees with gold. Up on the heights, battalions of black pines witnessed the change, themselves unchangeable. Either country was spanned by a cloudless sky, and in either arose the tinkle of church bells.

The garden of Windy Corners was deserted except for a red book, which lay sunning itself upon the gravel path. From the house came incoherent sounds, as of females preparing for worship.

``The men say they won't go'' (Minnie/Minnie Beebe)
--
``Well, I don't blame them'' (Minnie/Minnie Beebe)

``need she (Windy) go?'' (Minnie/Minnie Beebe)

``Dearest Lucia, may I trespass upon you for a pin?'' (Miss Bartlett/Charlotte Bartlett/Miss Charlotte Bartlett)
For Miss Bartlett had announced that she (Bartlett) at all events was one for church.

The sun rose higher on its journey, guided, not by Phaethon, but by Apollo, competent, unswerving, divine. Its rays fell on the ladies whenever they advanced towards the bedroom windows; on Mr. Beebe down at Summer Street as he (Beebe) smiled over a letter from Miss Catharine Alan; on George Emerson cleaning his (Emerson) father's boots; and lastly, to complete the catalogue of memorable things, on the red book mentioned previously. The ladies move, Mr. Beebe moves, George moves, and movement may engender shadow. But this book lies motionless, to be caressed all the morning by the sun and to raise its covers slightly, as though acknowledging the caress.

At her (Lucy) throat is a garnet brooch, on her (Lucy) finger a ring set with rubies--an engagement ring. Her (Lucy) eyes are bent to the Weald. She (Lucy) frowns a little--not in anger, but as a brave child frowns when he (Lucy) is trying not to cry. In all that expanse no human eye is looking at her (Lucy), and she (Lucy) may frown unrebuked and measure the spaces that yet survive between Apollo and the western hills.

Chapter XVI: Lying to George

But Lucy had developed since the spring. That is to say, she (Lucy) was now better able to stifle the emotions of which the conventions and the world disapprove. Though the danger was greater, she (Lucy) was not shaken by deep sobs. She (Lucy) said to Cecil,

She (Lucy) put out the lamp.

It did not do to think, nor, for the matter of that, to feel. She (Lucy) gave up trying to understand herself, and joined the vast armies of the benighted, who follow neither the heart nor the brain, and march to their destiny by catch-words. The armies are full of pleasant and pious folk. But they have yielded to the only enemy that matters--the enemy within. They have sinned against passion and truth, and vain will be their strife after virtue. As the years pass, they are censured. Their pleasantry and their piety show cracks, their wit becomes cynicism, their unselfishness hypocrisy; they feel and produce discomfort wherever they go. They have sinned against Eros and against Pallas Athene, and not by any heavenly intervention, but by the ordinary course of nature, those allied deities will be avenged.

Lucy entered this army when she (Lucy) pretended to George that she (Lucy) did not love him (George), and pretended to Cecil that she (Lucy) loved no one. The night received her (Lucy), as it had received Miss Bartlett thirty years before.

Chapter XVIII: Lying to Mr. Beebe, Mrs. Honeychurch, Freddy, and The Servants

Windy Corner lay, not on the summit of the ridge, but a few hundred feet down the southern slope, at the springing of one of the great buttresses that supported the hill. On either side of it was a shallow ravine, filled with ferns and pine-trees, and down the ravine on the left ran the highway into the Weald.

Whenever Mr. Beebe crossed the ridge and caught sight of these noble dispositions of the earth, and, poised in the middle of them, Windy Corner,--he (Beebe) laughed. The situation was so glorious, the house so commonplace, not to say impertinent. The late Mr. Honeychurch had affected the cube, because it gave him (Honeychurch) the most accommodation for his (Honeychurch) money, and the only addition made by his (Honeychurch) widow had been a small turret, shaped like a rhinoceros' horn, where she (widow) could sit in wet weather and watch the carts going up and down the road. So impertinent--and yet the house

``did,'' (people)
for it was the home of people who loved their surroundings honestly. Other houses in the neighborhood had been built by expensive architects, over others their inmates had fidgeted sedulously, yet all these suggested the accidental, the temporary; while Windy Corner seemed as inevitable as an ugliness of Nature's own creation. One might laugh at the house, but one never shuddered. Mr. Beebe was bicycling over this Monday afternoon with a piece of gossip. He (Beebe) had heard from the Miss Alans. These admirable ladies, since they could not go to Cissie Villa, had changed their plans. They were going to Greece instead.

``Since Florence did my poor sister so much good,'' (Miss Alan/Miss Catharine Alan/Miss Catharine)
wrote Miss Catharine,
``we do not see why we should not try Athens this winter. Of course, Athens is a plunge, and the doctor has ordered her (sister) special digestive bread; but, after all, we can take that with us, and it is only getting first into a steamer and then into a train. But is there an English Church?'' (Miss Alan/Miss Catharine Alan/Miss Catharine)
And the letter went on to say:
``I do not expect we shall go any further than Athens, but if you knew of a really comfortable pension at Constantinople, we should be so grateful.'' (Lucy/Miss Honeychurch/Honeychurch)

Lucy would enjoy this letter, and the smile with which Mr. Beebe greeted Windy Corner was partly for her (Windy). She (Windy) would see the fun of it, and some of its beauty, for she (Windy) must see some beauty. Though she (Windy) was hopeless about pictures, and though she (Windy) dressed so unevenly--oh, that cerise frock yesterday at church!--she (Windy) must see some beauty in life, or she (Windy) could not play the piano as she (Windy) did. He (Beebe) had a theory that musicians are incredibly complex, and know far less than other artists what they want and what they are; that they puzzle themselves as well as their friends; that their psychology is a modern development, and has not yet been understood. This theory, had he (Beebe) known it, had possibly just been illustrated by facts. Ignorant of the events of yesterday he (Beebe) was only riding over to get some tea, to see his (Beebe) niece, and to observe whether Miss Honeychurch saw anything beautiful in the desire of two old ladies to visit Athens.

A carriage was drawn up outside Windy Corner, and just as he (Beebe) caught sight of the house it started, bowled up the drive, and stopped abruptly when it reached the main road. Therefore it must be the horse, who always expected people to walk up the hill in case they tired him (Beebe). The door opened obediently, and two men emerged, whom Mr. Beebe recognized as Cecil and Freddy. They were an odd couple to go driving; but he (Beebe) saw a trunk beside the coachman's legs. Cecil, who wore a bowler, must be going away, while Freddy -LRB-a cap-RRB---was seeing him (Freddy) to the station. They walked rapidly, taking the short cuts, and reached the summit while the carriage was still pursuing the windings of the road.

They shook hands with the clergyman, but did not speak.

``So you're off for a minute, Mr. Vyse?'' (he)
he (clergyman) asked.

Cecil said,

``Yes,'' (Cecil)
while Freddy edged away.

``I was coming to show you this delightful letter from those friends of Miss Honeychurch.'' (He)

Cecil listened civilly, and said he (Cecil) was sure that Lucy would be amused and interested.

``Isn't Romance capricious! So they call it out of decency, but in their hearts they want a pension with magic windows opening on the foam of perilous seas in fairyland forlorn! No ordinary view will content the Miss Alans. Chapter XIX: Lying to Mr. Emerson

The Miss Alans were found in their beloved temperance hotel near Bloomsbury--a clean, airless establishment much patronized by provincial England. They always perched there before crossing the great seas, and for a week or two would fidget gently over clothes, guide-books, mackintosh squares, digestive bread, and other Continental necessaries. That there are shops abroad, even in Athens, never occurred to them, for they regarded travel as a species of warfare, only to be undertaken by those who have been fully armed at the Haymarket Stores. Miss Honeychurch, they trusted, would take care to equip herself duly. Quinine could now be obtained in tabloids; paper soap was a great help towards freshening up one's face in the train. Lucy promised, a little depressed.

``But, of course, you know all about these things, and you have Mr. Vyse to help you. A gentleman is such a stand-by.'' (Mrs. Honeychurch)

Mrs. Honeychurch, who had come up to town with her (Honeychurch) daughter, began to drum nervously upon her (Honeychurch) card-case.

said Miss Teresa, the more acute and less kindly of the two sisters.

``Oh, rubbish, Miss Honeychurch!'' (unknown)

``It is not rubbish!'' (the man)
said the old man hotly.

called voices from the carriage.

He (Beebe) looked amazed at the request, and said in a low, stern voice:

fired up the other again.

He (Beebe) walked out and left them. They heard him (Beebe) guiding his (Beebe) mother up-stairs.

``Lucy!'' (unknown)
the voices called.

She (mother) turned to Mr. Emerson in despair. But his (Emerson) face revived her (mother). It was the face of a saint who understood.

``Now it is all dark. Now Beauty and Passion seem never to have existed. I know. But remember the mountains over Florence and the view. Ah, dear, if I were George, and gave you one kiss, it would make you brave. You have to go cold into a battle that needs warmth, out into the muddle that you have made yourself; and your mother and all your friends will despise you, oh, my darling, and rightly, if it is ever right to despise.
